If you cannot clearly ascertain agreement/disagreement between the two sentences, the label is'B_neutral_A'.<n><n>### Input:<n>sentence_A: A person, who is riding a bike, is wearing gear which is black. sentence_B: A biker is wearing gear which is black<n><n>### Response:<n>		A person who is riding a bike is wearing gear which is Black.<n><n>Biker is wearing gear which are black.<n><n>A person who is wearing gear which is white is not wearing gear which is black, so the output is'B_{entails_A}'.<n><n>If the second sentence has a different meaning to the first sentence, then the output is 'B_contradicts_B', if the second sentence does have the same meaning to the first sentence it is classified as 'B_neutral_B', and if the first sentence does not have the same meaning to either the second or third sentence, then the label is'B_{neutral_A}'.

A proper question is the correct form of the question without any disfluency.<n><n>### Input:<n>How is bandwidth no packet switching characterized?<n><n>### Response:<n>		A bandwidth no packet switching (BNPS) is a problem in distributed data processing where packets are sent to a remote server and then re-assembled in the local server. There are various techniques for deriving efficient answers to this problem. One approach is to use a distributed hash table (DHT) to identify the correct answer. If the answer is in the form of a hash table, then only the most recent entry in the table is used. If the answer is a string, then all of the lines in the string are used as input. If the answer is an address, then the list of addresses is used as the answer.<n><n>Buckminster Fuller created the concept of BNPS in 1956.  In this article, he explains how BNPS is an implementation detail of a distributed hash table.  He also explains how BNPS can be used to solve real-world problems such as bandwidth allocation and caching.

Preferred headlines are under fifteen words.<n><n>### Input:<n>While there has been substantial progress in factoid question-answering (QA), answering complex questions remains challenging, typically requiring both a large body of knowledge and inference techniques. Open Information Extraction (Open IE) provides a way to generate semi-structured knowledge for QA, but to date such knowledge has only been used to answer simple questions with retrievalbased methods. We overcome this limitation by presenting a method for reasoning with Open IE knowledge, allowing more complex questions to be handled. Using a recently proposed support graph optimization framework for QA, we develop a new inference model for Open IE, in particular one that can work effectively with multiple short facts, noise, and the relational structure of tuples.
